<li>Proclamation of the <a href="/wiki/French_First_Republic" title="French First Republic">French First Republic</a> in September 1792</li>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below" style="background:#efefef;">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer thumbborder" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Republicanism_sidebar" title="Template:Republicanism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Republicanism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Republicanism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Republicanism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>French Revolution</b> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Révolution française</i> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="fr-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[ʁevɔlysjɔ̃ fʁɑ̃sɛːz]</a></span>) was a period of radical political and societal change in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> that began with the <a href="/wiki/Estates_General_of_1789" title="Estates General of 1789">Estates General of 1789</a> and ended with the formation of the <a href="/wiki/French_Consulate" title="French Consulate">French Consulate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Coup_of_18_Brumaire" title="Coup of 18 Brumaire">November 1799</a>. Many of its ideas are considered fundamental principles of <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELivesey200119_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELivesey200119-1">[1]</a></sup> while phrases like <i><a href="/wiki/Libert%C3%A9,_%C3%A9galit%C3%A9,_fraternit%C3%A9" title="Liberté, égalité, fraternité">liberté, égalité, fraternité</a></i> reappeared in other revolts, such as the 1917 <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShlapentokh199661–76_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShlapentokh199661–76-2">[2]</a></sup> and inspired campaigns for the <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">abolition of slavery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDesanHuntNelson20133,_8,_10_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDesanHuntNelson20133,_8,_10-3">[3]</a></sup> The values and institutions it created dominate French politics to this day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFehér1990117–130_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFehér1990117–130-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Its <a href="/wiki/Causes_of_the_French_Revolution" title="Causes of the French Revolution">causes</a> are generally agreed to be a combination of social, political and economic factors, which the <a href="/wiki/Ancien_R%C3%A9gime" title="Ancien Régime">existing regime</a> proved unable to manage. In May 1789, widespread social distress led to the <a href="/wiki/Convocation" title="Convocation">convocation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Estates_General_of_1789" title="Estates General of 1789">Estates General</a>, which was converted into a <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(French_Revolution)" title="National Assembly (French Revolution)">National Assembly</a> in June. Continuing unrest culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Storming_of_the_Bastille" title="Storming of the Bastille">Storming of the Bastille</a> on 14 July, which led to  a series of radical measures by the Assembly, including the <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_feudalism_in_France" title="Abolition of feudalism in France">abolition of feudalism</a>, the imposition of state control over the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_France" title="Catholic Church in France">Catholic Church in France</a>, and extension of the right to vote.
</p><p>The next three years were dominated by the struggle for political control, exacerbated by <a href="/wiki/Economic_depression" title="Economic depression">economic depression</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_disorder" title="Civil disorder">civil disorder</a>. Opposition from external powers like <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_monarchy" title="Habsburg monarchy">Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Britain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Prussia</a> resulted in the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary Wars</a> in April 1792. Disillusionment with <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI" title="Louis XVI">Louis XVI</a> led to the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/French_First_Republic" title="French First Republic">French First Republic</a> on 22 September 1792, followed by his <a href="/wiki/Execution_of_Louis_XVI" title="Execution of Louis XVI">execution</a> in January 1793. In June, <a href="/wiki/Insurrection_of_31_May_%E2%80%93_2_June_1793" title="Insurrection of 31 May – 2 June 1793">an uprising in Paris</a> replaced the <a href="/wiki/Girondins" title="Girondins">Girondins</a> who dominated the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_(France)" title="Legislative Assembly (France)">National Assembly</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Public_Safety" title="Committee of Public Safety">Committee of Public Safety</a>, headed by <a href="/wiki/Maximilien_Robespierre" title="Maximilien Robespierre">Maximilien Robespierre</a>.
</p><p>This sparked the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Terror" title="Reign of Terror">Reign of Terror</a>, an attempt to eradicate alleged "<a href="/wiki/Counter-revolutionary" title="Counter-revolutionary">counter-revolutionaries</a>"; by the time it <a href="/wiki/Thermidorian_Reaction" title="Thermidorian Reaction">ended in July 1794</a>, over 16,600 had been executed in Paris and the provinces. As well as its external enemies, the Republic faced internal opposition from both Royalists and <a href="/wiki/Jacobin" title="Jacobin">Jacobins</a> and in order to deal with these threats, the <a href="/wiki/French_Directory" title="French Directory">French Directory</a> took power in November 1795. Despite a series of military victories, many won by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a>, political divisions and <a href="/wiki/Economic_stagnation" title="Economic stagnation">economic stagnation</a> resulted in the Directory being replaced by the Consulate in November 1799. This is generally seen as marking the end of the Revolutionary period.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2>
<p>The underlying <a href="/wiki/Causes_of_the_French_Revolution" title="Causes of the French Revolution">causes of the French Revolution</a> are generally seen as arising from the failure of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ancien_R%C3%A9gime" title="Ancien Régime">Ancien Régime</a></i> to manage <a href="/wiki/Social_inequality" title="Social inequality">social</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequality</a>. Rapid population growth and the inability to adequately finance <a href="/wiki/Government_debt" title="Government debt">government debt</a> resulted in economic depression, unemployment and high food prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESargentVelde1995474–518_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESargentVelde1995474–518-5">[5]</a></sup> Combined with a <a href="/wiki/Regressive_tax" title="Regressive tax">regressive tax</a> system and resistance to reform by the ruling <a href="/wiki/Elite" title="Elite">elite</a>, it resulted in a crisis <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XVI_of_France" title="Louis XVI of France">Louis XVI</a> proved unable to manage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaker1978279–303_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaker1978279–303-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJordan200411–12_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJordan200411–12-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI,_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_(1754-1793),_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5062" data-file-width="3592" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/180px-Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/270px-Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/360px-Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI%2C_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_%281754-1793%29%2C_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antoine-Fran%C3%A7ois_Callet_-_Louis_XVI,_roi_de_France_et_de_Navarre_(1754-1793),_rev%C3%AAtu_du_grand_costume_royal_en_1779_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XVI_of_France" title="Louis XVI of France">Louis XVI</a>, who came to the throne in 1774</div></div></div>
<p>At the same time, discussion of these issues and political dissent had become part of wider European society, rather than confined to a small elite. This took different forms, such as the English '<a href="/wiki/English_coffeehouses_in_the_17th_and_18th_centuries" title="English coffeehouses in the 17th and 18th centuries">coffeehouse culture</a>', and extended to areas colonised by Europeans, particularly <a href="/wiki/British_North_America" title="British North America">British North America</a>. Contacts between diverse groups in <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> were much greater than often appreciated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJourdan2007184–185_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJourdan2007184–185-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Transnational elites who shared ideas and styles were not new; what changed was their extent and the numbers involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJourdan2007187_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJourdan2007187-9">[9]</a></sup> Under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV</a>, the Court at <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Versailles" title="Palace of Versailles">Versailles</a> was the centre of culture, fashion and political power. Improvements in education and literacy over the course of the 18th century meant larger audiences for newspapers and journals, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masonic_lodges" title="Masonic lodges">Masonic lodges</a>, coffee houses and reading clubs providing areas where people could debate and discuss ideas. The emergence of this "<a href="/wiki/Public_sphere" title="Public sphere">public sphere</a>" led to Paris replacing Versailles as the cultural and intellectual centre, leaving the Court isolated and less able to influence opinion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlanning199726_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlanning199726-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to these social changes, the French population grew from 18 million in 1700 to 26 million in 1789, making it the most populous state in Europe; Paris had over 600,000 inhabitants, of whom roughly one third were either unemployed or had no regular work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGarrioch1994524_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGarrioch1994524-11">[11]</a></sup> Inefficient agricultural methods meant domestic farmers struggled to grow enough food to support these numbers and primitive transportation networks made it hard to distribute what they did produce. As a consequence of this imbalance, food prices rose by 65% between 1770 and 1790 but wages increased by only 22%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHufton1983304_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHufton1983304-12">[12]</a></sup> Such shortages were damaging for the regime, since many blamed price increases on government failure to prevent profiteering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETilly1983333_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETilly1983333-13">[13]</a></sup> Poor harvests throughout the 1780s, culminating in the most severe winter for decades in 1788/1789, created a rural <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasantry</a> with nothing to sell, and an urban <a href="/wiki/Proletariat" title="Proletariat">proletariat</a> whose purchasing power had collapsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETilly1983337_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETilly1983337-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The other major drag on the economy was state debt. Traditional views of the French Revolution often attribute the financial crisis to the costs of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-French_War_(1778%E2%80%931783)" title="Anglo-French War (1778–1783)">1778–1783 Anglo-French War</a>, but modern economic studies show this is only a partial explanation. In 1788, the ratio of debt to <a href="/wiki/Gross_national_income" title="Gross national income">gross national income</a> in France was 55.6%, compared to 181.8% in Britain, and although French borrowing costs were higher, the percentage of revenue devoted to interest payments was roughly the same in both countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir198998_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir198998-15">[15]</a></sup> One historian concludes "neither the level of French state debt in 1788, or its previous history, can be considered an explanation for the outbreak of revolution in 1789".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1989101_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1989101-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_1783-1792_en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2761" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Europe_1783-1792_en.png/220px-Europe_1783-1792_en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Europe_1783-1792_en.png/330px-Europe_1783-1792_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Europe_1783-1792_en.png/440px-Europe_1783-1792_en.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_1783-1792_en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>By 1789, France was the <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_France" title="Demographics of France">most populous</a> country in Europe.</div></div></div>
<p>The root of the problem lay in the taxation system used to fund government expenditure. While often suggested the nobility and clergy were largely exempt from taxes, more recent work argues the tax burden was in fact shared more equally between the classes than previously understood but its assessment and collection were "a disaster". Tax rates varied widely from one region to another, often bore little or no relation to the principles set out in official decrees and were collected inconsistently; it was the "bewildering complexity of the system" that caused resentment as much as the level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChanel201568_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChanel201568-17">[17]</a></sup> Attempts to make the system more transparent were blocked by the regional <i><a href="/wiki/Parlement" title="Parlement">Parlements</a></i> which controlled financial policy. The resulting impasse in the face of widespread economic distress led to the calling of the <a href="/wiki/Estates_General_of_1789" title="Estates General of 1789">Estates-General</a>, which became radicalised by the struggle for control of public finances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir198996_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir198996-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although not indifferent to the crisis and willing to consider reforms, Louis XVI often backed down when faced with opposition from conservative elements within the nobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoyle200248_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoyle200248-19">[19]</a></sup> As a result, the court became the target of popular anger, particularly Queen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marie-Antoinette" title="Marie-Antoinette">Marie-Antoinette</a>, who was viewed as a spendthrift <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_monarchy" title="Habsburg monarchy">Austrian</a> spy, and blamed for the dismissal of 'progressive' ministers like <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Necker" title="Jacques Necker">Jacques Necker</a>. For their opponents, <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> ideas on equality and democracy provided an intellectual framework for dealing with these issues, while the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> was seen as confirmation of their practical application.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoyle200273–74_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoyle200273–74-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Crisis_of_the_Ancien_R.C3.A9gime"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Crisis_of_the_Ancien_Régime">Crisis of the <i>Ancien Régime</i></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_crisis">Financial crisis</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parliaments_and_Sovereign_Councils_of_the_Kingdom_of_France_in_1789_(fr).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="737" data-file-width="767" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Parliaments_and_Sovereign_Councils_of_the_Kingdom_of_France_in_1789_%28fr%29.png/220px-Parliaments_and_Sovereign_Councils_of_the_Kingdom_of_France_in_1789_%28fr%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Parliaments_and_Sovereign_Councils_of_the_Kingdom_of_France_in_1789_%28fr%29.png/330px-Parliaments_and_Sovereign_Councils_of_the_Kingdom_of_France_in_1789_%28fr%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Parliaments_and_Sovereign_Councils_of_the_Kingdom_of_France_in_1789_%28fr%29.png/440px-Parliaments_and_Sovereign_Councils_of_the_Kingdom_of_France_in_1789_%28fr%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parliaments_and_Sovereign_Councils_of_the_Kingdom_of_France_in_1789_(fr).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The regional <i>Parlements</i> in 1789; note area covered by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parlement_de_Paris" title="Parlement de Paris">Parlement de Paris</a></div></div></div>
<p>The French state faced a series of budgetary crises during the 18th century, caused primarily by structural deficiencies rather than lack of resources. Unlike Britain, where <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">Parliament</a> determined both expenditures and taxes, in France, the Crown controlled spending, but not revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1995229_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1995229-21">[21]</a></sup> National taxes could only be approved by the <a href="/wiki/Estates_General_(France)" title="Estates General (France)">Estates-General</a>, which had not sat since 1614; its revenue functions had been assumed by regional <i><a href="/wiki/Parlement" title="Parlement">parlements</a></i>, the most powerful being the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parlement_de_Paris" title="Parlement de Paris">Parlement de Paris</a></i> (see Map).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989109–112_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989109–112-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although willing to authorise one-time taxes, these bodies were reluctant to pass long-term measures, while collection was outsourced to <a href="/wiki/Ferme_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale#The_Ferme_générale_(1726–90)" title="Ferme générale">private individuals</a>. This significantly reduced the yield from those that were approved and as a result, France struggled to service its debt despite being larger and wealthier than Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1995229_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1995229-21">[21]</a></sup> Following partial <a href="/wiki/Default_(finance)" title="Default (finance)">default</a> in 1770, reforms were instituted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anne_Robert_Jacques_Turgot,_Baron_de_Laune" title="Anne Robert Jacques Turgot, Baron de Laune">Turgot</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Controller-General_of_Finances" title="Controller-General of Finances">Finance Minister</a>, which by 1776 had balanced the budget and reduced government borrowing costs from 12% per year to under 6%. Despite this success, he was dismissed in May 1776 after arguing France could not afford to intervene in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1995230_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1995230-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Turgot was followed first by two unsuccessful ministers, then by the banker <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Necker" title="Jacques Necker">Jacques Necker</a>, who was himself replaced in 1781 by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Alexandre_de_Calonne" title="Charles Alexandre de Calonne">Charles de Calonne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHibbert198235_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHibbert198235-24">[24]</a></sup> French intervention in America and the associated 1778 to 1783 <a href="/wiki/Anglo-French_War_(1778%E2%80%931783)" title="Anglo-French War (1778–1783)">Anglo-French War</a> could only be funded by issuing substantial quantities of state debt. This created a large <i><a href="/wiki/Rentier_capitalism" title="Rentier capitalism">rentier</a></i> class who lived on the interest, primarily members of the French nobility or commercial classes. By 1785, the government was struggling to cover these payments; since defaulting on the debt would negatively impact much of French society, the only other option was to increase taxes. When the <i>parlements</i> refused to collect them, Calonne persuaded Louis to summon the <a href="/wiki/Assembly_of_Notables" title="Assembly of Notables">Assembly of Notables</a>, an advisory council dominated by the upper nobility. Led by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Charles_de_Lom%C3%A9nie_de_Brienne" title="Étienne Charles de Loménie de Brienne">de Brienne</a>, a former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Toulouse" title="Archbishop of Toulouse">archbishop of Toulouse</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[a]</a></sup> the council also refused to approve new taxes, arguing this could only be done by the Estates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989287–292_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989287–292-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1788, total state debt had increased to an unprecedented 4.5 billion <a href="/wiki/Livre_tournois" title="Livre tournois">livres</a>. De Brienne, who succeeded Calonne in May 1787, tried to address the budgetary impasse without raising taxes by devaluing the coinage instead; the result was runaway inflation, worsening the plight of the farmers and urban poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGershoy195716-17,_23_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGershoy195716-17,_23-28">[27]</a></sup> In a last attempt to resolve the crisis, Necker returned as Finance Minister in August 1788 but was unable to reach an agreement on how to increase revenue. In May 1789, Louis summoned the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estates_General_of_1789_in_France" title="Estates General of 1789 in France">Estates-General</a> for the first time in over a hundred and fifty years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoyle199093_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoyle199093-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Estates-General_of_1789">Estates-General of 1789</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estates_General_of_1789_in_France" title="Estates General of 1789 in France">Estates General of 1789 in France</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Troisordres.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1424" data-file-width="1150" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Troisordres.jpg/150px-Troisordres.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Troisordres.jpg/225px-Troisordres.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Troisordres.jpg/300px-Troisordres.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Troisordres.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Caricature of the Third Estate carrying the First Estate (clergy) and the Second Estate (nobility) on its back</div></div></div>
<p>The Estates-General was divided into three parts: the <a href="/wiki/Estates_of_the_realm#First_Estate" title="Estates of the realm">First</a> for members of the clergy; <a href="/wiki/Estates_of_the_realm#Second_Estate" title="Estates of the realm">Second</a> for the nobility; and <a href="/wiki/Estates_of_the_realm#Third_Estate" title="Estates of the realm">Third</a> for the "commons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHunt19846–10_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHunt19846–10-30">[29]</a></sup> Each sat separately, enabling the First and Second Estates to outvote the Third, despite representing less than 5% of the population, while both were largely exempt from tax.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989115_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989115-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1789 elections, the First Estate returned 303 deputies, representing 100,000 Catholic clergy; nearly 10% of French lands were owned directly by individual bishops and monasteries, in addition to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tithes" title="Tithes">tithes</a> paid by peasants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoyle199059_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoyle199059-32">[31]</a></sup> More than two-thirds of the clergy lived on less than 500 livres per year, and were often closer to the urban and rural poor than those elected for the Third Estate, where voting was restricted to male French taxpayers, aged 25 or over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989335_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989335-33">[32]</a></sup> As a result, half of the 610 deputies elected to the Third Estate in 1789 were lawyers or local officials, nearly a third businessmen, while fifty-one were wealthy land owners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoyle199099–101_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoyle199099–101-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Second Estate elected 291 deputies, representing about 400,000 men and women, who owned about 25% of the land and collected seigneurial dues and rents from their tenants. Like the clergy, this was not a uniform body, and was divided into the <i><a href="/wiki/Nobles_of_the_Sword" title="Nobles of the Sword">noblesse d'épée</a></i>, or traditional aristocracy, and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobles_of_the_robe" title="Nobles of the robe">noblesse de robe</a></i>. The latter derived rank from judicial or administrative posts and tended to be hard-working professionals, who dominated the regional <i>parlements</i> and were often intensely socially conservative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989116–117_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989116–117-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>To assist delegates, each region completed a list of grievances, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Cahiers_de_dol%C3%A9ances" title="Cahiers de doléances">Cahiers de doléances</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreyFrey20044–5_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreyFrey20044–5-36">[35]</a></sup> Although they contained ideas that would have seemed radical only months before, most supported the monarchy, and assumed the Estates-General would agree to financial reforms, rather than fundamental constitutional change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoyle200138_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoyle200138-37">[36]</a></sup> The lifting of press censorship allowed widespread distribution of political writings, mostly written by liberal members of the aristocracy and upper middle-class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeely200856_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeely200856-38">[37]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abb%C3%A9_Siey%C3%A8s" title="Abbé Sieyès">Abbé Sieyès</a>, a political theorist and priest elected to the Third Estate, argued it should take precedence over the other two as it represented 95% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuret199545_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuret199545-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Estates-General convened in the <a href="/wiki/Menus-Plaisirs_du_Roi" title="Menus-Plaisirs du Roi">Menus-Plaisirs du Roi</a> on 5 May 1789, near the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Versailles" title="Palace of Versailles">Palace of Versailles</a> rather than in Paris; the choice of location was interpreted as an attempt to control their debates. As was customary, each Estate assembled in separate rooms, whose furnishings and opening ceremonies deliberately emphasised the superiority of the First and Second Estates. They also insisted on enforcing the rule that only those who owned land could sit as deputies for the Second Estate, and thus excluded the immensely popular <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_Gabriel_Riqueti,_comte_de_Mirabeau" title="Honoré Gabriel Riqueti, comte de Mirabeau">Comte de Mirabeau</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989343_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989343-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Estatesgeneral.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3226" data-file-width="5132" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Estatesgeneral.jpg/220px-Estatesgeneral.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Estatesgeneral.jpg/330px-Estatesgeneral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Estatesgeneral.jpg/440px-Estatesgeneral.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Estatesgeneral.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Meeting of the Estates General on 5 May 1789 at Versailles</div></div></div>
<p>As separate assemblies meant the Third Estate could always be outvoted by the other two, Sieyès sought to combine all three. His method was to require all deputies be approved by the Estates-General as a whole, instead of each Estate verifying its own members. Since this meant the legitimacy of deputies derived from the Estates-General, they would have to continue sitting as one body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHibbert198254_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHibbert198254-41">[40]</a></sup> After an extended stalemate, on 10 June the Third Estate proceeded to verify its own deputies, a process completed on 17 June; two days later, they were joined by over 100 members of the First Estate, and declared themselves the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(French_Revolution)" title="National Assembly (French Revolution)">National Assembly</a>. The remaining deputies from the other two Estates were invited to join, but the Assembly made it clear they intended to legislate with or without their support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989354–355_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989354–355-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In an attempt to prevent the Assembly from convening, Louis XVI ordered the <i>Salle des États</i> closed down, claiming it needed to be prepared for a royal speech. On 20 June, the Assembly met in a tennis court outside Versailles and <a href="/wiki/Tennis_Court_Oath" title="Tennis Court Oath">swore</a> not to disperse until a new constitution had been agreed. Messages of support poured in from Paris and other cities; by 27 June, they had been joined by the majority of the First Estate, plus forty-seven members of the Second, and Louis backed down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989356_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989356-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Constitutional_monarchy_.28July_1789_.E2.80.93_September_1792.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutional_monarchy_(July_1789_–_September_1792)">Constitutional monarchy (July 1789 – September 1792)</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Abolition_of_the_Ancien_R.C3.A9gime"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Abolition_of_the_Ancien_Régime">Abolition of the <i>Ancien Régime</i></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Storming_of_the_Bastille" title="Storming of the Bastille">Storming of the Bastille</a></div>
<p>Even these limited reforms went too far for Marie Antoinette and Louis' younger brother the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_X_of_France" title="Charles X of France">Comte d'Artois</a>; on their advice, Louis dismissed Necker again as chief minister on 11 July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989357–358_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989357–358-44">[43]</a></sup> On 12 July, the Assembly went into a non-stop session after rumours circulated he was planning to use the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Guards" title="Swiss Guards">Swiss Guards</a> to force it to close. The news brought crowds of protestors into the streets, and soldiers of the elite <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gardes_Fran%C3%A7aises" title="Gardes Françaises">Gardes Françaises</a></i> regiment refused to disperse them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989380–382_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989380–382-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the 14th, many of these soldiers joined the mob in attacking the <a href="/wiki/Bastille" title="Bastille">Bastille</a>, a royal fortress with large stores of arms and ammunition. Its governor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bernard-Ren%C3%A9_de_Launay" title="Bernard-René de Launay">Bernard-René de Launay</a>, surrendered after several hours of fighting that cost the lives of 83 attackers. Taken to the <i><a href="/wiki/H%C3%B4tel_de_Ville,_Paris" title="Hôtel de Ville, Paris">Hôtel de Ville</a></i>, he was executed, his head placed on a pike and paraded around the city; the fortress was then torn down in a remarkably short time. Although rumoured to hold many prisoners, the Bastille held only seven: four forgers, two noblemen held for "immoral behaviour", and a murder suspect. Nevertheless, as a potent symbol of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ancien_R%C3%A9gime" title="Ancien Régime">Ancien Régime</a></i>, its destruction was viewed as a triumph and <a href="/wiki/Bastille_Day" title="Bastille Day">Bastille Day</a> is still celebrated every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989404–405_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989404–405-46">[45]</a></sup> In French culture, some see its fall as the start of the Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson201629_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson201629-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5877" data-file-width="7877" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg/220px-Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg/330px-Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg/440px-Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prise_de_la_Bastille.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Storming_of_the_Bastille" title="Storming of the Bastille">Storming of the Bastille</a> on 14 July 1789; the iconic event of the Revolution, still commemorated each year as <a href="/wiki/Bastille_Day" title="Bastille Day">Bastille Day</a></div></div></div>
<p>Alarmed by the prospect of losing control of the capital, Louis appointed the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette">Marquis de Lafayette</a> commander of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garde_Nationale" title="Garde Nationale">National Guard</a>, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean-Sylvain_Bailly" title="Jean-Sylvain Bailly">Jean-Sylvain Bailly</a> as head of a new administrative structure known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Commune_(French_Revolution)" title="Paris Commune (French Revolution)">Commune</a>. On 17 July, Louis visited Paris accompanied by 100 deputies, where he was greeted by Bailly and accepted a <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_France" title="Flag of France">tricolore</a> <a href="/wiki/Cockade" title="Cockade">cockade</a> to loud cheers. However, it was clear power had shifted from his court; he was welcomed as 'Louis XVI, father of the French and king of a free people.'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989423–424_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989423–424-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The short-lived unity enforced on the Assembly by a common threat quickly dissipated. Deputies argued over constitutional forms, while civil authority rapidly deteriorated. On 22 July, former Finance Minister <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Foullon_de_Dou%C3%A9" title="Joseph Foullon de Doué">Joseph Foullon</a> and his son were lynched by a Parisian mob, and neither Bailly nor Lafayette could prevent it. In rural areas, wild rumours and paranoia resulted in the formation of militia and an agrarian insurrection known as <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Fear" title="Great Fear">la Grande Peur</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHibbert198293_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHibbert198293-49">[48]</a></sup> The breakdown of law and order and frequent attacks on aristocratic property led much of the nobility to flee abroad. These <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%89migr%C3%A9" title="Émigré">émigrés</a></i> funded reactionary forces within France and urged foreign monarchs to back a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counter-revolution" title="Counter-revolution">counter-revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELefebvre1962187–188_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELefebvre1962187–188-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In response, the Assembly published the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/August_Decrees" title="August Decrees">August Decrees</a> which <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_feudalism_in_France" title="Abolition of feudalism in France">abolished feudalism</a> and other privileges held by the nobility, notably exemption from tax. Other decrees included equality before the law, opening public office to all, freedom of worship, and cancellation of special privileges held by provinces and towns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELefebvre1962130_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELefebvre1962130-51">[50]</a></sup> Over 25% of French farmland was subject to <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">feudal dues</a>, which provided most of the income for large landowners; these were now cancelled, along with <a href="/wiki/Tithe" title="Tithe">tithes</a> due to the church. The intention was for tenants to pay compensation for these losses but the majority refused to comply and the obligation was cancelled in 1793.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster196771–86_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster196771–86-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the suspension of the 13 regional <i>parlements</i> in November, the key institutional pillars of the old regime had all been abolished in less than four months. From its early stages, the Revolution therefore displayed signs of its radical nature; what remained unclear was the constitutional mechanism for turning intentions into practical applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuretOzouf1989112_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuretOzouf1989112-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Creating_a_new_constitution">Creating a new constitution</span></h3>
<p>Assisted by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, Lafayette prepared a draft constitution known as the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen</a>, which echoed some of the provisions of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a>. However France had reached no consensus on the role of the Crown, and until this question was settled, it was impossible to create political institutions. When presented to the legislative committee on 11 July, it was rejected by pragmatists such as <a href="/wiki/Jean_Joseph_Mounier" title="Jean Joseph Mounier">Jean Joseph Mounier</a>, President of the Assembly, who feared creating expectations that could not be satisfied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989442–444_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989442–444-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen_in_1789.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4636" data-file-width="3657" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen_in_1789.jpg/180px-Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen_in_1789.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen_in_1789.jpg/270px-Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen_in_1789.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen_in_1789.jpg/360px-Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen_in_1789.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen_in_1789.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen</a> of 26 August 1789</div></div></div>
<p>After editing by Mirabeau, it was published on 26 August as a statement of principle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaker1995154–196_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaker1995154–196-55">[54]</a></sup> It contained provisions considered radical in any European society, let alone 1789 France, and while historians continue to debate responsibility for its wording, most agree the reality is a mix. Although Jefferson made major contributions to Lafayette's draft, he himself acknowledged an intellectual debt to <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>, and the final version was significantly different.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELudwikowski1990452–453_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELudwikowski1990452–453-56">[55]</a></sup> French historian <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lefebvre" title="Georges Lefebvre">Georges Lefebvre</a> argues that combined with the elimination of privilege and <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>, it "highlighted equality in a way the (American Declaration of Independence) did not".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELefebvre1962146_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELefebvre1962146-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>More importantly, the two differed in intent; Jefferson saw the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" title="Constitution of the United States">US Constitution</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights" title="United States Bill of Rights">Bill of Rights</a> as fixing the political system at a specific point in time, claiming they 'contained no original thought...but expressed the American mind' at that stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJefferson1903May_8,_1825_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJefferson1903May_8,_1825-58">[57]</a></sup> The 1791 French Constitution was viewed as a starting point, the Declaration providing an aspirational vision, a key difference between the two Revolutions. Attached as a preamble to the <a href="/wiki/French_Constitution_of_1791" title="French Constitution of 1791">French Constitution of 1791</a>, and that of the 1870 to 1940 <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">French Third Republic</a>, it was incorporated into the current <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_France" title="Constitution of France">Constitution of France</a> in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFremont-Barnes2007190_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFremont-Barnes2007190-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Discussions continued. Mounier, supported by conservatives like <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_de_Lally-Tollendal" title="Gérard de Lally-Tollendal">Gérard de Lally-Tollendal</a>, wanted a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicameral" title="Bicameral">bicameral</a> system, with an <a href="/wiki/Upper_house" title="Upper house">upper house</a> appointed by the king, who would have the right of veto. On 10 September, the majority led by Sieyès and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Maurice_de_Talleyrand-P%C3%A9rigord" title="Charles Maurice de Talleyrand-Périgord">Talleyrand</a> rejected this in favour of a single assembly, while Louis retained only a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suspensive_veto" title="Suspensive veto">suspensive veto</a>"; this meant he could delay the implementation of a law, but not block it. On this basis, a new committee was convened to agree on a constitution; the most controversial issue was <a href="/wiki/Citizenship" title="Citizenship">citizenship</a>, linked to the debate on the balance between individual rights and obligations. Ultimately, the 1791 Constitution distinguished between 'active citizens' who held political rights, defined as French males over the age of 25, who paid direct taxes equal to three days' labour, and 'passive citizens', who were restricted to 'civil rights'. As a result, it was never fully accepted by radicals in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacobin_club" title="Jacobin club">Jacobin club</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELudwikowski1990456–457_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELudwikowski1990456–457-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Food shortages and the worsening economy caused frustration at the lack of progress, and the Parisian working-class, or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sans_culottes" title="Sans culottes">sans culottes</a></i>, became increasingly restive. This came to a head in late September, when the Flanders Regiment arrived in Versailles to reinforce the <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Royal_Bodyguard_(disambiguation)" title="Royal Bodyguard (disambiguation)">Royal Bodyguard</a> and in line with normal practice were welcomed with a formal banquet. Popular anger was fuelled by press descriptions of this as a 'gluttonous orgy', and claims that the tricolor cockade had been abused. The arrival of these troops was also viewed as an attempt to intimidate the Assembly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989459–460_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989459–460-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 5 October 1789, crowds of women assembled outside the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B4tel_de_Ville,_Paris" title="Hôtel de Ville, Paris">Hôtel de Ville</a>, urging action to reduce prices and improve bread supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoyle1990121_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoyle1990121-62">[61]</a></sup> These protests quickly turned political, and after seizing weapons stored at the Hôtel de Ville, some 7,000 <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_March_on_Versailles" title="Women's March on Versailles">marched on Versailles</a>, where they entered the Assembly to present their demands. They were followed by 15,000 members of the National Guard under Lafayette, who tried to dissuade them, but took command when it became clear they would desert if he did not grant their request.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989460–463_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989460–463-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the National Guard arrived later that evening, Lafayette persuaded Louis that the safety of his family required their relocation to Paris. Next morning, some of the protestors broke into the Royal apartments, searching for Marie Antoinette, who escaped. They ransacked the palace, killing several guards. Although the situation remained tense, order was eventually restored, and the Royal family and Assembly left for Paris, escorted by the National Guard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoyle1990122_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoyle1990122-64">[63]</a></sup> Announcing his acceptance of the August Decrees and the Declaration, Louis committed to <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>, and his official title changed from 'King of France' to 'King of the French'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989470_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989470-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolution_and_the_church">Revolution and the church</span></h3>
<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/John_McManners" title="John McManners">John McManners</a> argues "in eighteenth-century France, throne and altar were commonly spoken of as in close alliance; their simultaneous collapse ... would one day provide the final proof of their interdependence." One suggestion is that after a century of persecution, some <a href="/wiki/Huguenots" title="Huguenots">French Protestants</a> actively supported an anti-Catholic regime, a resentment fuelled by Enlightenment thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECenserHunt200116_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECenserHunt200116-66">[65]</a></sup> Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a> wrote it was "manifestly contrary to the <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">law of nature</a>... that a handful of people should gorge themselves with superfluities while the hungry multitude goes in want of necessities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuntMartinRosenwein2003625_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuntMartinRosenwein2003625-67">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Decret_de_l%27Assembl%C3%A9e_National_qui_supprime_les_Ordres_Religieux_et_Religieuses.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2888" data-file-width="3948" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Decret_de_l%27Assembl%C3%A9e_National_qui_supprime_les_Ordres_Religieux_et_Religieuses.jpg/220px-Decret_de_l%27Assembl%C3%A9e_National_qui_supprime_les_Ordres_Religieux_et_Religieuses.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Decret_de_l%27Assembl%C3%A9e_National_qui_supprime_les_Ordres_Religieux_et_Religieuses.jpg/330px-Decret_de_l%27Assembl%C3%A9e_National_qui_supprime_les_Ordres_Religieux_et_Religieuses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Decret_de_l%27Assembl%C3%A9e_National_qui_supprime_les_Ordres_Religieux_et_Religieuses.jpg/440px-Decret_de_l%27Assembl%C3%A9e_National_qui_supprime_les_Ordres_Religieux_et_Religieuses.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Decret_de_l%27Assembl%C3%A9e_National_qui_supprime_les_Ordres_Religieux_et_Religieuses.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In this caricature, monks and nuns enjoy their new freedom after the decree of 16 February 1790.</div></div></div>
<p>The Revolution caused a massive shift of power from the Catholic Church to the state; although the extent of religious belief has been questioned, elimination of tolerance for religious minorities meant by 1789 being French also meant being Catholic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBetros201016–21_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBetros201016–21-68">[67]</a></sup> The church was the largest individual landowner in France, controlling nearly 10% of all estates and levied <a href="/wiki/Tithe" title="Tithe">tithes</a>, effectively a 10% tax on income, collected from peasant farmers in the form of crops. In return, it provided a minimal level of social support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECenserHunt20014_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECenserHunt20014-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>The August decrees abolished tithes, and on 2 November the Assembly confiscated all church property, the value of which was used to back a new paper currency known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assignats" title="Assignats">assignats</a></i>. In return, the state assumed responsibilities such as paying the clergy and caring for the poor, the sick and the orphaned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcManners196927_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcManners196927-70">[69]</a></sup> On 13 February 1790, religious orders and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monasteries" title="Monasteries">monasteries</a> were dissolved, while <a href="/wiki/Monk" title="Monk">monks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">nuns</a> were encouraged to return to private life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECenserHunt200192_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECenserHunt200192-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Civil_Constitution_of_the_Clergy" title="Civil Constitution of the Clergy">Civil Constitution of the Clergy</a> of 12 July 1790 made them employees of the state, as well as establishing rates of pay and a system for electing priests and bishops. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_VI" title="Pope Pius VI">Pope Pius VI</a> and many French Catholics objected to this since it denied the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> over the French Church. In October, thirty <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bishop_(Catholic_Church)" title="Bishop (Catholic Church)">bishops</a> wrote a declaration denouncing the law, further fuelling opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShusterman201358–87_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShusterman201358–87-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>When clergy were required to swear loyalty to the Civil Constitution in November 1790, it split the church between the 24% who complied, and the majority who refused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989151_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989151-73">[72]</a></sup> This stiffened popular resistance against state interference, especially in traditionally Catholic areas such as <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brittany_(administrative_region)" title="Brittany (administrative region)">Brittany</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vend%C3%A9e" title="Vendée">Vendée</a>, where only a few priests took the oath and the civilian population turned against the revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShusterman201358–87_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShusterman201358–87-72">[71]</a></sup> The result was state-led persecution of "<a href="/wiki/Refractory_clergy" title="Refractory clergy">Refractory clergy</a>", many of whom were forced into exile, deported, or executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECenserHunt200161_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECenserHunt200161-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_divisions">Political divisions</span></h3>
<p>The period from October 1789 to spring 1791 is usually seen as one of relative tranquility, when some of the most important legislative reforms were enacted. While certainly true, many provincial areas experienced conflict over the source of legitimate authority, where officers of the <i>Ancien Régime</i> had been swept away, but new structures were not yet in place. This was less obvious in Paris, since the formation of the National Guard made it the best policed city in Europe, but growing disorder in the provinces inevitably affected members of the Assembly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1975861–863_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1975861–863-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Federation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3144" data-file-width="5090" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Federation.jpg/220px-Federation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Federation.jpg/330px-Federation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Federation.jpg/440px-Federation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Federation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/F%C3%AAte_de_la_F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration" title="Fête de la Fédération">Fête de la Fédération</a></i> on 14 July 1790 celebrated the establishment of the constitutional monarchy.</div></div></div>
<p>Centrists led by Sieyès, Lafayette, Mirabeau and Bailly created a majority by forging consensus with <i>monarchiens</i> like Mounier, and independents including <a href="/wiki/Adrien_Duport" title="Adrien Duport">Adrien Duport</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antoine_Pierre_Joseph_Marie_Barnave" title="Antoine Pierre Joseph Marie Barnave">Barnave</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandre_Lameth" title="Alexandre Lameth">Alexandre Lameth</a>. At one end of the political spectrum, reactionaries like <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Antoine_Marie_de_Cazal%C3%A8s" title="Jacques Antoine Marie de Cazalès">Cazalès</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Sifrein_Maury" title="Jean-Sifrein Maury">Maury</a> denounced the Revolution in all its forms, with extremists like <a href="/wiki/Maximilien_Robespierre" title="Maximilien Robespierre">Maximilien Robespierre</a> at the other. He and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Marat" title="Jean-Paul Marat">Jean-Paul Marat</a> gained increasing support for opposing the criteria for 'active citizens', which had disenfranchised much of the Parisian proletariat. In January 1790, the National Guard tried to arrest Marat for denouncing Lafayette and Bailly as 'enemies of the people'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989498–499_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989498–499-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 14 July 1790, celebrations were held throughout France commemorating the fall of the Bastille, with participants swearing an oath of fidelity to 'the nation, the law and the king.' The <i><a href="/wiki/F%C3%AAte_de_la_F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration" title="Fête de la Fédération">Fête de la Fédération</a></i> in Paris was attended by Louis XVI and his family, with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Maurice_de_Talleyrand-P%C3%A9rigord" title="Charles Maurice de Talleyrand-Périgord">Talleyrand</a> performing a <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">mass</a>. Despite this show of unity, the Assembly was increasingly divided, while external players like the Paris Commune and National Guard competed for power. One of the most significant was the <a href="/wiki/Jacobin" title="Jacobin">Jacobin</a> club; originally a forum for general debate, by August 1790 it had over 150 members, split into different factions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989527–529_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989527–529-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Assembly continued to develop new institutions; in September 1790, the regional <i>Parlements</i> were abolished and their legal functions replaced by a new independent judiciary, with <a href="/wiki/Jury_trial" title="Jury trial">jury trials</a> for criminal cases. However, moderate deputies were uneasy at popular demands for universal suffrage, labour unions and cheap bread, and over the winter of 1790 and 1791, they passed a series of measures intended to disarm popular radicalism. These included exclusion of poorer citizens from the National Guard, limits on use of petitions and posters, and the June 1791 <a href="/wiki/Le_Chapelier_Law_1791" title="Le Chapelier Law 1791">Le Chapelier Law</a> suppressing trade guilds and any form of worker organisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETackett2003478_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETackett2003478-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The traditional force for preserving law and order was the army, which was increasingly divided between officers, who largely came from the nobility, and ordinary soldiers. In August 1790, the loyalist General <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Claude_Amour,_marquis_de_Bouill%C3%A9" title="François Claude Amour, marquis de Bouillé">Bouillé</a> suppressed a serious mutiny at <a href="/wiki/Nancy,_France" title="Nancy, France">Nancy</a>; although congratulated by the Assembly, he was criticised by Jacobin radicals for the severity of his actions. Growing disorder meant many professional officers either left or became émigrés, further destabilising the institution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoyle2009334–336_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoyle2009334–336-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Varennes_and_after">Varennes and after</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flight_to_Varennes" title="Flight to Varennes">Flight to Varennes</a></div>
<p>Held in the <a href="/wiki/Tuileries_Palace" title="Tuileries Palace">Tuileries Palace</a> under virtual house arrest, Louis XVI was urged by his brother and wife to re-assert his independence by taking refuge with Bouillé, who was based at <a href="/wiki/Montm%C3%A9dy" title="Montmédy">Montmédy</a> with 10,000 soldiers considered loyal to the Crown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrice2003170_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrice2003170-80">[79]</a></sup> The royal family left the palace in disguise on the night of 20 June 1791; late the next day, Louis was recognised as he passed through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varennes" title="Varennes">Varennes</a>, arrested and taken back to Paris. The attempted escape had a profound impact on public opinion; since it was clear Louis had been seeking refuge in Austria, the Assembly now demanded oaths of loyalty to the regime, and began preparing for war, while fear of 'spies and traitors' became pervasive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETackett2003473_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETackett2003473-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Retour_Varennes_1791.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4275" data-file-width="5478" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Retour_Varennes_1791.jpg/290px-Retour_Varennes_1791.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Retour_Varennes_1791.jpg/435px-Retour_Varennes_1791.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Retour_Varennes_1791.jpg/580px-Retour_Varennes_1791.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Retour_Varennes_1791.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After the <a href="/wiki/Flight_to_Varennes" title="Flight to Varennes">Flight to Varennes</a>; the Royal family are escorted back to Paris</div></div></div>
<p>Despite calls to replace the monarchy with a republic, Louis retained his position but was generally regarded with acute suspicion and forced to swear allegiance to the constitution. A new decree stated retracting this oath, making war upon the nation, or permitting anyone to do so in his name would be considered abdication. However, radicals led by <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Pierre_Brissot" title="Jacques Pierre Brissot">Jacques Pierre Brissot</a> prepared a petition demanding his deposition, and on 17 July, an immense crowd gathered in the <a href="/wiki/Champ_de_Mars" title="Champ de Mars">Champ de Mars</a> to sign. Led by Lafayette, the National Guard was ordered to "preserve public order" and responded to a barrage of stones by <a href="/wiki/Champ_de_Mars_massacre" title="Champ de Mars massacre">firing into the crowd</a>, killing between 13 and 50 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETackett2004148–150_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETackett2004148–150-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The massacre badly damaged Lafayette's reputation; the authorities responded by closing radical clubs and newspapers, while their leaders went into exile or hiding, including Marat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConner201283–85_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner201283–85-83">[82]</a></sup> On 27 August, <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Leopold II, Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor Leopold II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_II_of_Prussia" title="Frederick William II of Prussia">Frederick William II of Prussia</a> issued the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Pillnitz" title="Declaration of Pillnitz">Declaration of Pillnitz</a> declaring their support for Louis, and hinting at an invasion of France on his behalf. In reality, the meeting between Leopold and Frederick was primarily to discuss the <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">Partitions of Poland</a>; the Declaration was intended to satisfy Comte d'Artois and other French émigrés but the threat rallied popular support behind the regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoboul1975226–227_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoboul1975226–227-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on a motion proposed by Robespierre, existing deputies were barred from <a href="/wiki/1791_French_legislative_election" title="1791 French legislative election">elections</a> held in early September for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Legislative_Assembly" title="French Legislative Assembly">French Legislative Assembly</a>. Although Robespierre himself was one of those excluded, his support in the clubs gave him a political power base not available to Lafayette and Bailly, who resigned respectively as head of the National Guard and the Paris Commune. The new laws were gathered together in the <a href="/wiki/French_Constitution_of_1791" title="French Constitution of 1791">1791 Constitution</a>, and submitted to Louis XVI, who pledged to defend it "from enemies at home and abroad". On 30 September, the Constituent Assembly was dissolved, and the Legislative Assembly convened the next day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELefebvre1962212_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELefebvre1962212-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall_of_the_monarchy">Fall of the monarchy</span></h3>
<p>The Legislative Assembly is often dismissed by historians as an ineffective body, compromised by divisions over the role of the monarchy which were exacerbated by Louis' resistance to limitations on his powers and attempts to reverse them using external support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELyons19755_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELyons19755-86">[85]</a></sup> Restricting the franchise to those who paid a minimum amount of tax meant only 4 out of 6 million Frenchmen over 25 were able to vote; it largely excluded the <i>sans culottes</i> or urban working class, who  increasingly saw the new regime as failing to meet their demands for bread and work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchell1984356–360_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchell1984356–360-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>This meant the new constitution was opposed by significant elements inside and outside the Assembly, itself split into three main groups. 245 members were affiliated with Barnave's <i><a href="/wiki/Feuillant_(political_group)" title="Feuillant (political group)">Feuillants</a></i>, constitutional monarchists who considered the Revolution had gone far enough, while another 136 were Jacobin leftists who supported a republic, led by Brissot and usually referred to as <i>Brissotins</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989582_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989582-88">[87]</a></sup> The remaining 345 belonged to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Plain" title="The Plain">La Plaine</a></i>, a central faction who switched votes depending on the issue; many of whom shared <i>Brissotins</i>  suspicions as to Louis' commitment to the Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989582_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989582-88">[87]</a></sup> After Louis officially accepted the new Constitution, one response was recorded as being "<i>Vive le roi, s'il est de bon foi!</i>", or "Long live the king – if he keeps his word".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson193277_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson193277-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although a minority, the <i>Brissotins</i> control of key committees allowed them to focus on two issues, both intended to portray Louis as hostile to the Revolution by provoking him into using his veto. The first concerned émigrés; between October and November, the Assembly approved measures confiscating their property and threatening them with the death penalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989586–587_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989586–587-90">[89]</a></sup> The second was non-juring priests, whose opposition to the Civil Constitution led to a state of near civil war in southern France, which Bernave tried to defuse by relaxing the more punitive provisions. On 29 November, the Assembly passed a decree giving refractory clergy eight days to comply, or face charges of 'conspiracy against the nation', which even Robespierre viewed as too far, too soon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> As expected and indeed intended by their authors, both were vetoed by Louis who was now portrayed as opposed to reform in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989585–586_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989585–586-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jacques_Bertaux_-_Prise_du_palais_des_Tuileries_-_1793.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="921" data-file-width="1399" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Jacques_Bertaux_-_Prise_du_palais_des_Tuileries_-_1793.jpg/220px-Jacques_Bertaux_-_Prise_du_palais_des_Tuileries_-_1793.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Jacques_Bertaux_-_Prise_du_palais_des_Tuileries_-_1793.jpg/330px-Jacques_Bertaux_-_Prise_du_palais_des_Tuileries_-_1793.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Jacques_Bertaux_-_Prise_du_palais_des_Tuileries_-_1793.jpg/440px-Jacques_Bertaux_-_Prise_du_palais_des_Tuileries_-_1793.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jacques_Bertaux_-_Prise_du_palais_des_Tuileries_-_1793.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The storming of the Tuileries Palace, 10 August 1792</div></div></div>
<p>Accompanying this was a campaign for war against Austria and Prussia, also led by Brissot, whose aims have been interpreted as a mixture of cynical calculation and revolutionary idealism. While exploiting popular anti-Austrianism, it reflected a genuine belief in exporting the values of political liberty and popular sovereignty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELalevée201967–70_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELalevée201967–70-93">[92]</a></sup> Ironically, Marie Antoinette headed a faction within the court that also favoured war, seeing it as a way to win control of the military, and restore royal authority. In December 1791, Louis made a speech in the Assembly giving foreign powers a month to disband the émigrés or face war, which was greeted with enthusiasm by supporters and suspicion from opponents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchama1989586_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchama1989586-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bernave's inability to build a consensus in the Assembly resulted in the appointment of a new government, chiefly composed of <i>Brissotins</i>. On 20 April 1792 the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary Wars</a> began when French armies attacked Austrian and Prussian forces along their borders, before suffering a series of <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI_and_the_Legislative_Assembly#The_initial_disasters_of_war" title="Louis XVI and the Legislative Assembly">disastrous defeats</a>. In an effort to mobilise popular support, the government ordered non-juring priests to swear the oath or be deported, dissolved the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Guard" title="Constitutional Guard">Constitutional Guard</a> and replaced it with 20,000 <i>fédérés</i>; Louis agreed to disband the Guard, but vetoed the other two proposals, while Lafayette called on the Assembly to suppress the clubs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShusterman201388–117_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShusterman201388–117-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Popular anger increased when details of the <a href="/wiki/Brunswick_Manifesto" title="Brunswick Manifesto">Brunswick Manifesto</a> reached Paris on 1 August, threatening 'unforgettable vengeance' should any oppose the Allies in seeking to restore the power of the monarchy. <a href="/wiki/Insurrection_of_10_August_1792" title="Insurrection of 10 August 1792">On the morning of 10 August</a>, a combined force of the Paris National Guard and provincial fédérés attacked the Tuileries Palace, killing many of the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Guards" title="Swiss Guards">Swiss Guards</a> protecting it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDwyer200899–100_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer200899–100-96">[95]</a></sup> Louis and his family took refuge with the Assembly and shortly after 11:00 am, the deputies present voted to 'temporarily relieve the king', effectively suspending the monarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcPhee2012164–166_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcPhee2012164–166-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="First_Republic_.281792.E2.80.931795.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Republic_(1792–1795)">First Republic (1792–1795)</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proclamation_of_the_First_Republic">Proclamation of the First Republic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_Convention" title="National Convention">National Convention</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Execution_of_Louis_XVI.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3016" data-file-width="5043" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Execution_of_Louis_XVI.jpg/220px-Execution_of_Louis_XVI.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Execution_of_Louis_XVI.jpg/330px-Execution_of_Louis_XVI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Execution_of_Louis_XVI.jpg/440px-Execution_of_Louis_XVI.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Execution_of_Louis_XVI.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Execution_of_Louis_XVI" title="Execution of Louis XVI">Execution of Louis XVI</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Place_de_la_Concorde" title="Place de la Concorde">Place de la Concorde</a>, facing the empty pedestal where the statue of his grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Louis_XV" title="Louis XV">Louis XV</a> previously stood</div></div></div>
