<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marche-des-marseillois.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3016" data-file-width="2882" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Marche-des-marseillois.jpg/200px-Marche-des-marseillois.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Marche-des-marseillois.jpg/300px-Marche-des-marseillois.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Marche-des-marseillois.jpg/400px-Marche-des-marseillois.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marche-des-marseillois.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marche des Marseillois, 1792, satirical etching, London<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[173]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/La_Marseillaise" title="La Marseillaise">La Marseillaise</a>" (<small>French pronunciation: ​</small><span class="IPA" lang="fr-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[la maʁsɛjɛːz]</a></span>) became the <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national anthem</a> of France. The song was written and composed in 1792 by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Joseph_Rouget_de_Lisle" title="Claude Joseph Rouget de Lisle">Claude Joseph Rouget de Lisle</a>, and was originally titled "<i>Chant de guerre pour l'Armée du Rhin</i>". The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_National_Convention" title="French National Convention">French National Convention</a> adopted it as the <a href="/wiki/French_First_Republic" title="French First Republic">First Republic's</a> anthem in 1795. It acquired its nickname after being sung in Paris by <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9r%C3%A9" title="Fédéré">volunteers</a> from <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a> marching on the capital.
</p><p>The song is the first example of the "European march" anthemic style, while the evocative melody and lyrics led to its widespread use as a song of revolution and incorporation into many pieces of classical and popular music. De Lisle was instructed to 'produce a hymn which conveys to the soul of the people the enthusiasm which it (the music) suggests.'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECerulo1993243–271_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECerulo1993243–271-177">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Guillotine">Guillotine</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cruikshank_-_The_Radical%27s_Arms.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="573" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Cruikshank_-_The_Radical%27s_Arms.png/130px-Cruikshank_-_The_Radical%27s_Arms.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Cruikshank_-_The_Radical%27s_Arms.png/195px-Cruikshank_-_The_Radical%27s_Arms.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Cruikshank_-_The_Radical%27s_Arms.png/260px-Cruikshank_-_The_Radical%27s_Arms.png 2x" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cruikshank_-_The_Radical%27s_Arms.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cartoon attacking the excesses of the Revolution as symbolised by the guillotine</div></div></div>
<p>The guillotine remains "the principal symbol of the Terror in the French Revolution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanson2007151_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanson2007151-178">[175]</a></sup> Invented by a physician during the Revolution as a quicker, more efficient and more distinctive form of execution, the guillotine became a part of popular culture and historic memory. It was celebrated on the left as the people's avenger, for example in the revolutionary song <i><a href="/wiki/La_guillotine_permanente" title="La guillotine permanente">La guillotine permanente</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDelonLevayer1989153–154_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDelonLevayer1989153–154-179">[176]</a></sup> and cursed as the symbol of the Terror by the right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuntMartinRosenwein2003664_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuntMartinRosenwein2003664-180">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Its operation became a popular entertainment that attracted great crowds of spectators. Vendors sold programmes listing the names of those scheduled to die. Many people came day after day and vied for the best locations from which to observe the proceedings; knitting women (<a href="/wiki/Tricoteuse" title="Tricoteuse">tricoteuses</a>) formed a cadre of hardcore regulars, inciting the crowd. Parents often brought their children. By the end of the Terror, the crowds had thinned drastically. Repetition had staled even this most grisly of entertainments, and audiences grew bored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Cockade.2C_tricolore_and_liberty_cap"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cockade,_tricolore_and_liberty_cap">Cockade, <i>tricolore</i> and liberty cap</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sans-culotte.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1455" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Sans-culotte.jpg/150px-Sans-culotte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Sans-culotte.jpg/225px-Sans-culotte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Sans-culotte.jpg/300px-Sans-culotte.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sans-culotte.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sans-culotte" title="Sans-culotte">sans-culotte</a></i> and Tricoloure</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cockade" title="Cockade">Cockades</a> were widely worn by revolutionaries beginning in 1789. They now pinned the blue-and-red cockade of Paris onto the white cockade of the <i>Ancien Régime</i>. <a href="/wiki/Camille_Desmoulins" title="Camille Desmoulins">Camille Desmoulins</a> asked his followers to wear green cockades on 12 July 1789. The Paris militia, formed on 13 July, adopted a blue and red cockade. Blue and red are the traditional colours of Paris, and they are used on the city's coat of arms. Cockades with various colour schemes were used during the storming of the Bastille on 14 July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrowdy200442_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrowdy200442-182">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Liberty cap, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Phrygian_cap" title="Phrygian cap">Phrygian cap</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Pileus_(hat)" title="Pileus (hat)">pileus</a>, is a brimless, felt cap that is conical in shape with the tip pulled forward. It reflects Roman republicanism and liberty, alluding to the Roman ritual of <a href="/wiki/Manumission" title="Manumission">manumission</a>, in which a freed slave receives the bonnet as a symbol of his newfound liberty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarden199566–102_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarden199566–102-183">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_women">Role of women</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_French_Revolution" title="Women in the French Revolution">Women in the French Revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Militant_feminism_in_the_French_Revolution" title="Militant feminism in the French Revolution">Militant feminism in the French Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ch%C3%A9rieux_-_Club_des_femmes_patriotes_dans_une_%C3%A9glise_-_1793.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1902" data-file-width="2554" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ch%C3%A9rieux_-_Club_des_femmes_patriotes_dans_une_%C3%A9glise_-_1793.jpg/180px-Ch%C3%A9rieux_-_Club_des_femmes_patriotes_dans_une_%C3%A9glise_-_1793.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ch%C3%A9rieux_-_Club_des_femmes_patriotes_dans_une_%C3%A9glise_-_1793.jpg/270px-Ch%C3%A9rieux_-_Club_des_femmes_patriotes_dans_une_%C3%A9glise_-_1793.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ch%C3%A9rieux_-_Club_des_femmes_patriotes_dans_une_%C3%A9glise_-_1793.jpg/360px-Ch%C3%A9rieux_-_Club_des_femmes_patriotes_dans_une_%C3%A9glise_-_1793.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ch%C3%A9rieux_-_Club_des_femmes_patriotes_dans_une_%C3%A9glise_-_1793.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Club of patriotic women in a church</div></div></div>
<p>The role of women in the Revolution has long been a topic of debate. Deprived of political rights under the <i>Ancien Regime</i>, the 1791 Constitution classed them as "passive" citizens, leading to demands for social and political equality for women and an end to male domination. They expressed these demands using pamphlets and clubs such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Friends_of_Truth" title="Society of the Friends of Truth">Cercle Social</a></i>, whose largely male members viewed themselves as contemporary feminists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHunt1996123_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHunt1996123-184">[181]</a></sup> However, in October 1793, the Assembly banned all women's clubs and the movement was crushed; this was driven by the emphasis on masculinity in a wartime situation, antagonism towards feminine "interference" in state affairs due to Marie Antoinette, and traditional male supremacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDevance1977341–376_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDevance1977341–376-185">[182]</a></sup> A decade later the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a> confirmed and perpetuated women's second-class status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbray197543–62_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbray197543–62-186">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the beginning of the Revolution, women took advantage of events to force their way into the political sphere, swore oaths of loyalty, "solemn declarations of patriotic allegiance, [and] affirmations of the political responsibilities of citizenship." Activists included Girondists like <a href="/wiki/Olympe_de_Gouges" title="Olympe de Gouges">Olympe de Gouges</a>, author of the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Woman_and_of_the_Female_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Woman and of the Female Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Woman and of the Female Citizen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Corday" title="Charlotte Corday">Charlotte Corday</a>, the killer of Marat. Others like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9roigne_de_M%C3%A9ricourt" title="Théroigne de Méricourt">Théroigne de Méricourt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pauline_L%C3%A9on" title="Pauline Léon">Pauline Léon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Revolutionary_Republican_Women" title="Society of Revolutionary Republican Women">Society of Revolutionary Republican Women</a> supported the Jacobins, staged demonstrations in the National Assembly and took part in the October 1789 March to Versailles. Despite this, the constitutions of 1791 and 1793 denied them political rights and democratic citizenship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelzerRabine199279_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelzerRabine199279-187">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 20 June 1792 a number of armed women took part in a procession that "passed through the halls of the Legislative Assembly, into the <a href="/wiki/Tuileries_Garden" title="Tuileries Garden">Tuileries Garden</a>, and then through the King's residence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelzerRabine199291_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelzerRabine199291-188">[185]</a></sup> Women also assumed a special role in the funeral of Marat, following his murder on 13 July 1793 by Corday; as part of the funeral procession, they carried the bathtub in which he died, as well as a shirt stained with his blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHufton199231_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHufton199231-189">[186]</a></sup> On 20 May 1793 women were in the forefront of a crowd demanding "bread and the Constitution of 1793"; when they went unnoticed, they began "sacking shops, seizing grain and kidnapping officials."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcMillan199924_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcMillan199924-190">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Olympe_de_Gouges.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1090" data-file-width="816" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Olympe_de_Gouges.png/180px-Olympe_de_Gouges.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Olympe_de_Gouges.png/270px-Olympe_de_Gouges.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Olympe_de_Gouges.png/360px-Olympe_de_Gouges.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Olympe_de_Gouges.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Olympe_de_Gouges" title="Olympe de Gouges">Olympe de Gouges</a>, Girondist author of the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Woman_and_of_the_Female_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Woman and of the Female Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Woman and of the Female Citizen</a>, executed in November 1793</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Revolutionary_Republican_Women" title="Society of Revolutionary Republican Women">Society of Revolutionary Republican Women</a>, a militant group on the far left, demanded a law in 1793 that would compel all women to wear the tricolour cockade to demonstrate their loyalty to the Republic. They also demanded vigorous price controls to keep bread – the major food of the poor people – from becoming too expensive. After the Convention passed the law in September 1793, the Revolutionary Republican Women demanded vigorous enforcement, but were countered by market women, former servants, and religious women who adamantly opposed price controls (which would drive them out of business) and resented attacks on the aristocracy and on religion. Fist fights broke out in the streets between the two factions of women.
</p><p>Meanwhile, the men who controlled the Jacobins rejected the Revolutionary Republican Women as dangerous rabble-rousers. At this point the Jacobins controlled the government; they dissolved the Society of Revolutionary Republican Women, and decreed that all women's clubs and associations were illegal. They sternly reminded women to stay home and tend to their families by leaving public affairs to the men. Organised women were permanently shut out of the French Revolution after 30 October 1793.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevyApplewhiteJohnson1979143–149_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevyApplewhiteJohnson1979143–149-191">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prominent_women">Prominent women</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Olympe_de_Gouges" title="Olympe de Gouges">Olympe de Gouges</a> wrote a number of plays, short stories, and novels. Her publications emphasised that women and men are different, but this shouldn't prevent equality under the law. In her <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Woman_and_of_the_Female_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Woman and of the Female Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Woman and of the Female Citizen</a> she insisted that women deserved rights, especially in areas concerning them directly, such as divorce and recognition of illegitimate children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Gouges_Writings_564–568_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-De_Gouges_Writings_564–568-192">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Madame_Roland" title="Madame Roland">Madame Roland</a> (a.k.a. Manon or Marie Roland) was another important female activist. Her political focus was not specifically on women or their liberation. She focused on other aspects of the government, but was a feminist by virtue of the fact that she was a woman working to influence the world. Her personal letters to leaders of the Revolution influenced policy; in addition, she often hosted political gatherings of the Brissotins, a political group which allowed women to join. As she was led to the scaffold, Madame Roland shouted "O liberty! What crimes are committed in thy name!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalton2001262–267_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalton2001262–267-193">[190]</a></sup> Many activists were punished for their actions, while some were executed for "conspiring against the unity and the indivisibility of the Republic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckstrand200920_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckstrand200920-194">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Counter-revolutionary_women">Counter-revolutionary women</span></h3>
<p>Counter-revolutionary women resisted what they saw as the increasing intrusion of the state into their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHufton1992104_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHufton1992104-195">[192]</a></sup> One major consequence was the <a href="/wiki/Dechristianization_of_France_during_the_French_Revolution" title="Dechristianization of France during the French Revolution">dechristianisation</a> of France, a movement strongly rejected by many devout people; especially for women living in rural areas, the closing of the churches meant a loss of normality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHufton1992106–107_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHufton1992106–107-196">[193]</a></sup> This sparked a counter-revolutionary movement led by women; while supporting other political and social changes, they opposed the dissolution of the Catholic Church and revolutionary cults like the <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_the_Supreme_Being" title="Cult of the Supreme Being">Cult of the Supreme Being</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDesanHuntNelson2013452_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDesanHuntNelson2013452-197">[194]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Olwen_Hufton" title="Olwen Hufton">Olwen Hufton</a> argues some wanted to protect the Church from heretical changes enforced by revolutionaries, viewing themselves as "defenders of faith".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHufton1998303_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHufton1998303-198">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>Economically, many peasant women refused to sell their goods for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assignats" title="Assignats">assignats</a> because this form of currency was unstable and was backed by the sale of confiscated Church property. By far the most important issue to counter-revolutionary women was the passage and the enforcement of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Constitution_of_the_Clergy" title="Civil Constitution of the Clergy">Civil Constitution of the Clergy</a> in 1790. In response to this measure, women in many areas began circulating anti-oath pamphlets and refused to attend masses held by priests who had sworn oaths of loyalty to the Republic. These women continued to adhere to traditional practices such as Christian burials and naming their children after saints in spite of revolutionary decrees to the contrary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHufton1998303–304_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHufton1998303–304-199">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_policies">Economic policies</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:France-500Livres-1790-uni.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="830" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/France-500Livres-1790-uni.jpg/220px-France-500Livres-1790-uni.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/France-500Livres-1790-uni.jpg/330px-France-500Livres-1790-uni.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/France-500Livres-1790-uni.jpg/440px-France-500Livres-1790-uni.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:France-500Livres-1790-uni.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early <i>Assignat</i> of 29 September 1790: 500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_livres" title="French livres">livres</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Revolution abolished many economic constraints imposed by the <i>Ancien régime</i>, including church tithes and feudal dues although tenants often paid higher rents and taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland20021–24_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland20021–24-200">[197]</a></sup> All church lands were nationalised, along with those owned by Royalist exiles, which were used to back paper currency known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assignats" title="Assignats">assignats</a>, and the feudal <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guild</a> system eliminated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVardi1988704–717_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVardi1988704–717-201">[198]</a></sup> It also abolished the highly inefficient system of <a href="/wiki/Ferme_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale" title="Ferme générale">tax farming</a>, whereby private individuals would collect taxes for a hefty fee. The government seized the foundations that had been set up (starting in the 13th century) to provide an annual stream of revenue for hospitals, poor relief, and education. The state sold the lands but typically local authorities did not replace the funding and so most of the nation's charitable and <a href="/wiki/History_of_education_in_France#Revolution" title="History of education in France">school systems were massively disrupted</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer1986181–197_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1986181–197-202">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1790 and 1796, industrial and agricultural output dropped, foreign trade plunged, and prices soared, forcing the government to finance expenditure by issuing ever increasing quantities <i>assignats</i>. When this resulted in escalating inflation, the response was to impose price controls and persecute private speculators and traders, creating a <a href="/wiki/Black_market" title="Black market">Black market</a>. Between 1789 and 1793, the annual deficit increased from 10% to 64% of gross national product, while annual inflation reached 3,500% after a poor harvest in 1794 and the removal of price controls. The assignats were withdrawn in 1796 but inflation continued until the introduction of the gold-based <i>Franc germinal</i> in 1803.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrezisCrouzet19957–40_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrezisCrouzet19957–40-203">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-term_impact">Long-term impact</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Influence_of_the_French_Revolution" title="Influence of the French Revolution">Influence of the French Revolution</a></div>
<p>The French Revolution had a major impact on European and Western history, by ending feudalism and creating the path for future advances in broadly defined individual freedoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmerColton1995341_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmerColton1995341-204">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFehér1990117–130_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFehér1990117–130-4">[4]</a></sup> Its impact on <a href="/wiki/French_nationalism" title="French nationalism">French nationalism</a> was profound, while also stimulating nationalist movements throughout Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDannDinwiddy198813_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDannDinwiddy198813-205">[202]</a></sup> Modern historians argue the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation state</a> was a direct consequence of the Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeitner200712_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeitner200712-206">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span></h3>
<p>The impact of the Revolution on French society was enormous and led to numerous changes, some of which were widely accepted, while others continue to be debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart1951783–94_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart1951783–94-207">[204]</a></sup> Under Louis XIV, political power was centralised at Versailles and controlled by the monarch, whose power derived from immense personal wealth, control over the army and appointment of clergy, provincial governors, lawyers and judges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson195222_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson195222-208">[205]</a></sup> In less than a year, the king was reduced to a figurehead, the nobility deprived of titles and estates and the church of its monasteries and property. Clergy, judges and magistrates were controlled by the state, and the army sidelined, with military power placed held by the revolutionary National Guard. The central elements of 1789 were the slogan "Liberty, Equality and Fraternity" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_the_Citizen" title="The Declaration of the Rights of Man and the Citizen">The Declaration of the Rights of Man and the Citizen</a>", which Lefebvre calls "the incarnation of the Revolution as a whole."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELefebvre1947212_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELefebvre1947212-209">[206]</a></sup>
</p><p>The long-term impact on France was profound, shaping politics, society, religion and ideas, and polarising politics for more than a century. Historian <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Victor_Alphonse_Aulard" title="François Victor Alphonse Aulard">François Aulard</a> writes:
</p>
<blockquote><p>"From the social point of view, the Revolution consisted in the suppression of what was called the feudal system, in the emancipation of the individual, in greater division of landed property, the abolition of the privileges of noble birth, the establishment of equality, the simplification of life.... The French Revolution differed from other revolutions in being not merely national, for it aimed at benefiting all humanity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[207]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title='Please supply either "|first=" and the actual title of the cited work, or use "|title=[untitled]" for intentional omission. (November 2020)'>title missing</span></a></i>]</sup></p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_of_the_Catholic_church">Status of the Catholic church</span></h4>
<p>One of the most heated controversies during the Revolution was the status of the Catholic Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989145–167_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989145–167-211">[208]</a></sup> In 1788, it held a dominant position within society; to be French meant to be a Catholic. By 1799, much of its property and institutions had been confiscated and its senior leaders dead or in exile. Its cultural influence was also under attack, with efforts made to strip civil life of religious elements such as Sundays, holy days, saints, prayers, rituals and ceremonies. Ultimately these attempts not only failed but aroused a furious reaction among the pious; opposition to these changes was a key factor behind the revolt in the Vendée.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989338–353_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989338–353-212">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Le_Bataillon_Carr%C3%A9,_Affaire_de_Foug%C3%A8res_1793_(Square_Battalion).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Le_Bataillon_Carr%C3%A9%2C_Affaire_de_Foug%C3%A8res_1793_%28Square_Battalion%29.jpg/220px-Le_Bataillon_Carr%C3%A9%2C_Affaire_de_Foug%C3%A8res_1793_%28Square_Battalion%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Le_Bataillon_Carr%C3%A9%2C_Affaire_de_Foug%C3%A8res_1793_%28Square_Battalion%29.jpg/330px-Le_Bataillon_Carr%C3%A9%2C_Affaire_de_Foug%C3%A8res_1793_%28Square_Battalion%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Le_Bataillon_Carr%C3%A9%2C_Affaire_de_Foug%C3%A8res_1793_%28Square_Battalion%29.jpg/440px-Le_Bataillon_Carr%C3%A9%2C_Affaire_de_Foug%C3%A8res_1793_%28Square_Battalion%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Le_Bataillon_Carr%C3%A9,_Affaire_de_Foug%C3%A8res_1793_(Square_Battalion).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1793 <a href="/wiki/War_in_the_Vend%C3%A9e" title="War in the Vendée">War in the Vendée</a> was in part sparked by opposition to state persecution of the Catholic church</div></div></div>
<p>Over the centuries, charitable foundations had been set up to fund hospitals, poor relief, and schools; when these were confiscated and sold off, the funding was not replaced, causing massive disruption to these support systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutherland20021–24_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutherland20021–24-200">[197]</a></sup> Under the <i>Ancien régime</i>, medical assistance for the rural poor was often provided by nuns, acting as nurses but also physicians, surgeons, and apothecaries; the Revolution abolished most of these orders without replacing organised nursing support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcHugh2012428–456_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcHugh2012428–456-213">[210]</a></sup> Demand remained strong and after 1800 nuns resumed their work in hospitals and on rural estates. They were tolerated by officials because they had widespread support and were a link between elite male physicians and distrustful peasants who needed help.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELéonard1977887–907_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELéonard1977887–907-214">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>The church was a primary target during the Terror, due to its association with "counter-revolutionary" elements, resulting in the persecution of priests and destruction of churches and religious images throughout France. An effort was made to replace the Catholic Church altogether with the <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_Reason" title="Cult of Reason">Cult of Reason</a>, and with civic festivals replacing religious ones, leading to attacks by locals on state officials. These policies were promoted by the atheist Hébert and opposed by the deist Robespierre, who denounced the campaign and replaced the Cult of Reason with the <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_the_Supreme_Being" title="Cult of the Supreme Being">Cult of the Supreme Being</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECenserHunt200192–94_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECenserHunt200192–94-215">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1801" title="Concordat of 1801">Concordat of 1801</a> established the rules for a relationship between the Catholic Church and French State that lasted until it was abrogated by the <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">French Third Republic</a> on 11 December 1905. The Concordat was a compromise that restored some of the Church's traditional roles but not its power, lands or monasteries; the clergy became public officials controlled by Paris, not Rome, while Protestants and Jews gained equal rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis1997235–255_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1997235–255-216">[213]</a></sup> However, debate continues into the present over the role of religion in the public sphere and related issues such as church-controlled schools. Recent arguments over the use of Muslim religious symbols in schools, such as wearing headscarves, have been explicitly linked to the conflict over Catholic rituals and symbols during the Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESoperFetzer200339–59_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESoperFetzer200339–59-217">[214]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h4>
<p>Two thirds of France was employed in agriculture, which was transformed by the Revolution. With the breakup of large estates controlled by the Church and the nobility and worked by hired hands, rural France became more a land of small independent farms. Harvest taxes were ended, such as the tithe and seigneurial dues, much to the relief of the peasants. <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">Primogeniture</a> was ended both for nobles and peasants, thereby weakening the family patriarch, and led to a fall in the born rate since all children had a share in the family property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1988251–54,_265_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1988251–54,_265-218">[215]</a></sup> Cobban argues the Revolution bequeathed to the nation "a ruling class of landowners."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECobban196489_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196489-219">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the cities, entrepreneurship on a small scale flourished, as restrictive monopolies, privileges, barriers, rules, taxes and guilds gave way. However, the British blockade virtually ended overseas and colonial trade, hurting the cities and their supply chains. Overall, the Revolution did not greatly change the French business system, and probably helped freeze in place the horizons of the small business owner. The typical businessman owned a small store, mill or shop, with family help and a few paid employees; large-scale industry was less common than in other industrialising nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECobban196468–80_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECobban196468–80-220">[217]</a></sup>
</p><p>Economic historians dispute the impact on income per capita caused by the emigration of more than 100,000 individuals during the Revolution, the vast majority of whom were supporters of the old regime. One suggestion is the resulting fragmentation of agricultural holdings had a significant negative impact in the early years of 19th century, then became positive in the second half of the century because it facilitated the rise in human capital investments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFranckMichalopoulos2017_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFranckMichalopoulos2017-221">[218]</a></sup> Others argue the redistribution of land had an immediate positive impact on agricultural productivity, before the scale of these gains gradually declined over the course of the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinleyFranckJohnson2017_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinleyFranckJohnson2017-222">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutionalism">Constitutionalism</span></h4>
<p>The Revolution meant an end to arbitrary royal rule and held out the promise of rule by law under a constitutional order, but it did not rule out a monarch. Napoleon as emperor set up a constitutional system (although he remained in full control), and the restored Bourbons were forced to go along with one. After the abdication of Napoleon III in 1871, the monarchists probably had a voting majority, but they were so factionalised they could not agree on who should be king, and instead the <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">French Third Republic</a> was launched with a deep commitment to upholding the ideals of the Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[221]</a></sup> The conservative Catholic enemies of the Revolution came to power in <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy France</a> (1940–44), and tried with little success to undo its heritage, but they kept it a republic. Vichy denied the principle of equality and tried to replace the Revolutionary watchwords "<a href="/wiki/Libert%C3%A9,_%C3%A9galit%C3%A9,_fraternit%C3%A9" title="Liberté, égalité, fraternité">Liberty, Equality, Fraternity</a>" with "Work, Family, and Fatherland." However, there were no efforts by the Bourbons, Vichy or anyone else to restore the privileges that had been stripped away from the nobility in 1789. France permanently became a society of equals under the law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanson2009189_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanson2009189-225">[222]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communism">Communism</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Jacobin" title="Jacobin">Jacobin</a> cause was picked up by <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxists</a> in the mid-19th century and became an element of <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communist thought</a> around the world. In the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gracchi" title="Gracchi">Gracchus</a>" <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-No%C3%ABl_Babeuf" title="François-Noël Babeuf">Babeuf</a> was regarded as a hero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[223]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe_outside_France">Europe outside France</span></h3>
<p>Economic historians Dan Bogart, Mauricio Drelichman, Oscar Gelderblom, and Jean-Laurent Rosenthal described <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codified law</a> as the French Revolution's "most significant export." They wrote, "While restoration returned most of their power to the absolute monarchs who had been deposed by Napoleon, only the most recalcitrant ones, such as Ferdinand VII of Spain, went to the trouble of completely reversing the legal innovations brought on by the French."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-227">[224]</a></sup> They also note that the French Revolution and the Napoleonic Wars caused England, Spain, Prussia and the Dutch Republic to centralize their fiscal systems to an unprecedented extent in order to finance the military campaigns of the Napoleonic Wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_227-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-227">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Daron_Acemoglu" title="Daron Acemoglu">Daron Acemoglu</a>, Davide Cantoni, <a href="/wiki/Simon_Johnson_(economist)" title="Simon Johnson (economist)">Simon Johnson</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_A._Robinson_(economist)" title="James A. Robinson (economist)">James A. Robinson</a> the French Revolution had long-term effects in Europe. They suggest that "areas that were occupied by the French and that underwent radical institutional reform experienced more rapid <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a> and economic growth, especially after 1850. There is no evidence of a negative effect of French invasion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2016 study in the <i><a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Review" title="European Economic Review">European Economic Review</a></i> found that the areas of Germany that were occupied by France in the 19th century and in which the Code Napoleon was applied have higher levels of trust and cooperation today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[226]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Britain">Britain</span></h4>
<p>On 16 July 1789, two days after the <a href="/wiki/Storming_of_the_Bastille" title="Storming of the Bastille">Storming of the Bastille</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Frederick_Sackville" title="John Frederick Sackville">John Frederick Sackville</a>, serving as ambassador to France, reported to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs">Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs</a> <a href="/wiki/Francis_Osborne,_5th_Duke_of_Leeds" title="Francis Osborne, 5th Duke of Leeds">Francis Osborne, 5th Duke of Leeds</a>, "Thus, my Lord, the greatest revolution that we know anything of has been effected with, comparatively speaking – if the magnitude of the event is considered – the loss of very few lives. From this moment we may consider France as a free country, the King a very limited monarch, and the nobility as reduced to a level with the rest of the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[227]</a></sup>" Yet in Britain the majority, especially among the aristocracy, strongly opposed the French Revolution. Britain led and funded the series of coalitions that fought France from 1793 to 1815, and then restored the Bourbons.
</p><p>Philosophically and politically, Britain was in debate over the rights and wrongs of revolution, in the abstract and in practicalities. The <a href="/wiki/Revolution_Controversy" title="Revolution Controversy">Revolution Controversy</a> was a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pamphlet_war" title="Pamphlet war">pamphlet war</a>" set off by the publication of <i><a href="/wiki/A_Discourse_on_the_Love_of_Our_Country" title="A Discourse on the Love of Our Country">A Discourse on the Love of Our Country</a></i>, a speech given by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Price" title="Richard Price">Richard Price</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Revolution_Society" title="Revolution Society">Revolution Society</a> on 4 November 1789, supporting the French Revolution (as he had the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>), and saying that patriotism actually centers around loving the people and principles of a nation, not its ruling class. <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> responded in November 1790 with his own pamphlet, <i><a href="/wiki/Reflections_on_the_Revolution_in_France" title="Reflections on the Revolution in France">Reflections on the Revolution in France</a></i>, attacking the French Revolution as a threat to the aristocracy of all countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[229]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=William_Coxe_(MP)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="William Coxe (MP) (page does not exist)">William Coxe</a> opposed Price's premise that one's country is principles and people, not the State itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark2000233_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark2000233-233">[230]</a></sup>
</p><p>Conversely, two seminal political pieces of political history were written in Price's favour, supporting the general right of the French people to replace their State. One of the first of these "<a href="/wiki/Pamphlet#History" title="Pamphlet">pamphlets</a>" into print was <i><a href="/wiki/A_Vindication_of_the_Rights_of_Men" title="A Vindication of the Rights of Men">A Vindication of the Rights of Men</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Mary_Wollstonecraft" title="Mary Wollstonecraft">Mary Wollstonecraft</a> (better known for her later treatise, sometimes described as the first feminist text, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Vindication_of_the_Rights_of_Woman" title="A Vindication of the Rights of Woman">A Vindication of the Rights of Woman</a></i>); Wollstonecraft's title was echoed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Rights_of_Man" title="Rights of Man">Rights of Man</a></i>, published a few months later. In 1792 <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wyvill" title="Christopher Wyvill">Christopher Wyvill</a> published <i>Defence of Dr. Price and the Reformers of England</i>, a plea for reform and moderation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[231]</a></sup>
</p><p>This exchange of ideas has been described as "one of the great political debates in British history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrowe200593_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrowe200593-235">[232]</a></sup> Even in France, there was a varying degree of agreement during this debate, English participants generally opposing the violent means that the Revolution bent itself to for its ends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[233]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Ireland, the effect was to transform what had been an attempt by Protestant settlers to gain some autonomy into a mass movement led by the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_United_Irishmen" title="Society of United Irishmen">Society of United Irishmen</a> involving Catholics and Protestants. It stimulated the demand for further reform throughout Ireland, especially in <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>. The upshot was a revolt in 1798, led by <a href="/wiki/Wolfe_Tone" title="Wolfe Tone">Wolfe Tone</a>, that was crushed by Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPelling20025–10_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPelling20025–10-237">[234]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span></h4>
<p>German reaction to the Revolution swung from favourable to antagonistic. At first it brought liberal and democratic ideas, the end of guilds, serfdom and the Jewish ghetto. It brought economic freedoms and agrarian and legal reform. Above all the antagonism helped stimulate and shape <a href="/wiki/German_nationalism" title="German nationalism">German nationalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[235]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland">Switzerland</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Helvetic_Republic" title="Helvetic Republic">Helvetic Republic</a></div>
<p>The French invaded Switzerland and turned it into the "<a href="/wiki/Helvetic_Republic" title="Helvetic Republic">Helvetic Republic</a>" (1798–1803), a French puppet state. French interference with localism and traditions was deeply resented in Switzerland, although some reforms took hold and survived in the later <a href="/wiki/Restoration_and_Regeneration_in_Switzerland" title="Restoration and Regeneration in Switzerland">period of restoration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[237]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgium">Belgium</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/French_period" title="French period">French period</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paul-Jacob_Laminit_(inc.)_Jahann_Voeltz_(dis.)_Combat_dans_les_rues_de_Gand,_nov%C3%A9mbre_1789.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2576" data-file-width="3772" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Paul-Jacob_Laminit_%28inc.%29_Jahann_Voeltz_%28dis.%29_Combat_dans_les_rues_de_Gand%2C_nov%C3%A9mbre_1789.JPG/220px-Paul-Jacob_Laminit_%28inc.%29_Jahann_Voeltz_%28dis.%29_Combat_dans_les_rues_de_Gand%2C_nov%C3%A9mbre_1789.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Paul-Jacob_Laminit_%28inc.%29_Jahann_Voeltz_%28dis.%29_Combat_dans_les_rues_de_Gand%2C_nov%C3%A9mbre_1789.JPG/330px-Paul-Jacob_Laminit_%28inc.%29_Jahann_Voeltz_%28dis.%29_Combat_dans_les_rues_de_Gand%2C_nov%C3%A9mbre_1789.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Paul-Jacob_Laminit_%28inc.%29_Jahann_Voeltz_%28dis.%29_Combat_dans_les_rues_de_Gand%2C_nov%C3%A9mbre_1789.JPG/440px-Paul-Jacob_Laminit_%28inc.%29_Jahann_Voeltz_%28dis.%29_Combat_dans_les_rues_de_Gand%2C_nov%C3%A9mbre_1789.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paul-Jacob_Laminit_(inc.)_Jahann_Voeltz_(dis.)_Combat_dans_les_rues_de_Gand,_nov%C3%A9mbre_1789.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Brabant_Revolution" title="Brabant Revolution">Brabant Revolution</a> broke out in the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Netherlands" title="Austrian Netherlands">Austrian Netherlands</a> in October 1789, inspired by the revolution in neighbouring France, but had collapsed by the end of 1790.</div></div></div>
<p>The region of modern-day Belgium was divided between two polities: the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Netherlands" title="Austrian Netherlands">Austrian Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prince-Bishopric_of_Li%C3%A8ge" title="Prince-Bishopric of Liège">Prince-Bishopric of Liège</a>. Both territories experienced revolutions in 1789. In the Austrian Netherlands, the <a href="/wiki/Brabant_Revolution" title="Brabant Revolution">Brabant Revolution</a> succeeded in expelling Austrian forces and established the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_of_Belgium" title="United States of Belgium">United Belgian States</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A8ge_Revolution" title="Liège Revolution">Liège Revolution</a> expelled the tyrannical Prince-Bishop and installed <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Li%C3%A8ge" title="Republic of Liège">a republic</a>. Both failed to attract international support. By December 1790, the Brabant revolution had been crushed and Liège was subdued the following year.
</p><p>During the Revolutionary Wars, the French invaded and occupied the region between 1794 and 1814, a time known as the <a href="/wiki/French_period" title="French period">French period</a>. The new government enforced new reforms, incorporating the region into France itself. New rulers were sent in by Paris. Belgian men were drafted into the French wars and heavily taxed. Nearly everyone was Catholic, but the Church was repressed. Resistance was strong in every sector, as Belgian nationalism emerged to oppose French rule. The French legal system, however, was adopted, with its equal legal rights, and abolition of class distinctions. Belgium now had a government bureaucracy selected by merit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKossmann197865–81,_101–02_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKossmann197865–81,_101–02-241">[238]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a> regained access to the sea and grew quickly as a major port and business centre. France promoted commerce and capitalism, paving the way for the ascent of the <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a> and the rapid growth of manufacturing and mining. In economics, therefore, the nobility declined while middle-class Belgian entrepreneurs flourished because of their inclusion in a large market, paving the way for Belgium's leadership role after 1815 in the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> on the Continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECook200449–54_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECook200449–54-242">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark1984140–75_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark1984140–75-243">[240]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Denmark#Reforms" title="History of Denmark">History of Denmark § Reforms</a></div>
<p>The Kingdom of Denmark adopted liberalising reforms in line with those of the French Revolution, with no direct contact. Reform was gradual and the regime itself carried out <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_reform" title="Agrarian reform">agrarian reforms</a> that had the effect of weakening absolutism by creating a class of independent peasant <a href="/wiki/Freehold_(law)" title="Freehold (law)">freeholders</a>. Much of the initiative came from well-organised liberals who directed political change in the first half of the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorstbollOstergård1990155–179_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorstbollOstergård1990155–179-244">[241]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Norway" title="Constitution of Norway">Constitution of Norway</a> of 1814 was inspired by the French Revolution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[242]</a></sup> and was considered to be one of the most liberal and democratic constitutions at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[243]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span></h4>
<p>Coverage of the Revolution in the then <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Quebec_(1763%E2%80%931791)" title="Province of Quebec (1763–1791)">Province of Quebec</a> took place against the background of an ongoing campaign for constitutional reform by <a href="/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution)" title="Loyalist (American Revolution)">Loyalist</a> emigrants from the United States. With the press reliant on reprinting articles from British newspapers, local opinion followed them in being generally positive on the aims and objectives of the revolutionaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwood199357–58_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwood199357–58-247">[244]</a></sup> This made it increasingly difficult to justify the withholding of electoral rights, with the British <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Grenville" title="William Grenville">William Grenville</a> remarking it was difficult to deny "to so large a body of British Subjects, the benefits of the British Constitution". This led to the "<a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Act_1791" title="Constitutional Act 1791">Constitutional Act 1791</a>", which split the Province into two separate colonies, each with its own electoral assembly, the predominantly French-speaking <a href="/wiki/Lower_Canada" title="Lower Canada">Lower Canada</a> and predominantly English-speaking <a href="/wiki/Upper_Canada" title="Upper Canada">Upper Canada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwood199363_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwood199363-248">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p>French migration into <a href="/wiki/The_Canadas" title="The Canadas">the Canadas</a> significantly declined during and after the Revolution, with only limited numbers of artisans, professionals, and religious emigres permitted to settle in that period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canimmg_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-canimmg-249">[246]</a></sup> Most emigres settled in <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">Quebec City</a>, although French nobleman <a href="/wiki/Joseph-Genevi%C3%A8ve_de_Puisaye" title="Joseph-Geneviève de Puisaye">Joseph-Geneviève de Puisaye</a> and a small group of Royalists settled lands north of <a href="/wiki/York,_Upper_Canada" title="York, Upper Canada">York</a>, modern day <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canimmg_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-canimmg-249">[246]</a></sup> The influx of religious migrants also reinvigorated the local Catholic Church, with exiled priests establishing a number of parishes throughout the Canadas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canimmg_249-2"><a href="#cite_note-canimmg-249">[246]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h4>
<p>The French Revolution deeply polarised American politics, and this polarisation led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">First Party System</a>. In 1793, as war broke out in Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> led by former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_France" title="United States Ambassador to France">American minister to France</a> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> favored revolutionary France and pointed to the 1778 treaty that was still in effect. <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> and his unanimous cabinet, including Jefferson, decided that the treaty did not bind the United States to enter the war. Washington <a href="/wiki/Proclamation_of_Neutrality" title="Proclamation of Neutrality">proclaimed neutrality</a> instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[247]</a></sup> Under President <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist</a>, an undeclared naval war took place with France from 1798 until 1799, often called the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quasi_War" title="Quasi War">Quasi War</a>". Jefferson became president in 1801, but was hostile to Napoleon as a dictator and emperor. However, the two entered negotiations over the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Territory" title="Louisiana Territory">Louisiana Territory</a> and agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> in 1803, an acquisition that substantially increased the size of the United States.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_the_French_Revolution" title="Historiography of the French Revolution">Historiography of the French Revolution</a></div>
<p>The French Revolution has received enormous amounts of historical attention, both from the general public as well as scholars and academics, while perspectives on its significance and major developments have often been characterised as falling along ideological lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERude199112–14_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERude199112–14-251">[248]</a></sup> In general, studies of the Revolution initially focused on political ideas and developments, but gradually shifted towards <a href="/wiki/Social_history" title="Social history">social history</a> that analyses its impact on individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERude199114–20_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERude199114–20-252">[249]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary conservatives like <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_von_Gentz" title="Friedrich von Gentz">Friedrich von Gentz</a> argued it was the product of a few conspiratorial individuals who brainwashed the masses into subverting the old order, a claim rooted in the belief that the revolutionaries had no legitimate complaints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERude199112_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERude199112-253">[250]</a></sup> In the 19th century, the Revolution was heavily analysed by economists and political scientists like <a href="/wiki/Alexis_de_Tocqueville" title="Alexis de Tocqueville">Alexis de Tocqueville</a>, who suggested it was the result of a more prosperous middle class becoming conscious of its social importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERude199115_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERude199115-254">[251]</a></sup> Perhaps the most influential was <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, who viewed the <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social class</a> nature of the Revolution as fundamental to understanding human social evolution itself. He argued the egalitarian values it introduced gave rise to a classless and co-operative model for society called "<a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>", which found direct expression in the 1870 to 1871 <a href="/wiki/Paris_Commune" title="Paris Commune">Paris Commune</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarx1983505–507_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarx1983505–507-255">[252]</a></sup>
</p><p>For much of the 20th century, historians influenced by Marx, notably <a href="/wiki/Albert_Soboul" title="Albert Soboul">Albert Soboul</a>, emphasised the role of the peasants and urban workers in the Revolution and presented it as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class_struggle" title="Class struggle">class struggle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERude199117_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERude199117-256">[253]</a></sup> The central theme of this argument was that the Revolution emerged from the rising bourgeoisie, with support from the <a href="/wiki/Sans-culottes" title="Sans-culottes">sans-culottes</a>, who united to destroy the aristocracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEComninel198731_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEComninel198731-257">[254]</a></sup> However, Western scholars largely abandoned Marxist interpretations in the 1990s; the theme of class conflict was widely discredited, but no new explanatory model has gained widespread support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpang2003119–147_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpang2003119–147-258">[255]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBell2004323–351_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell2004323–351-259">[256]</a></sup> Nevertheless, in Western history the Revolution is still seen as a key dividing point between the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Late_modern_period" title="Late modern period">late modern</a> periods, and thus one of its most important events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpang2003119–147_258-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpang2003119–147-258">[255]</a></sup>
</p><p>Within France itself, the Revolution permanently crippled the power of the aristocracy and drained the wealth of the Church, although the two institutions survived despite the damage they sustained. After the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/First_French_Empire" title="First French Empire">First French Empire</a> in 1815, the French public lost many of the rights and privileges earned since the Revolution, but remembered the participatory politics that characterised the period. According to one historian: "Thousands of men and even many women gained firsthand experience in the political arena: they talked, read, and listened in new ways; they voted; they joined new organisations; and they marched for their political goals. Revolution became a tradition, and <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a> an enduring option."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanson2009189_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanson2009189-225">[222]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is also suggested the French underwent a fundamental transformation in self-identity, evidenced by the elimination of privileges and their replacement by intrinsic <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>, as well as a decline in social deference that highlighted the principle of equality throughout the Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanson2009191_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanson2009191-260">[257]</a></sup> The Revolution represented the most significant and dramatic challenge to political absolutism up to that point in history and spread democratic ideals throughout Europe and ultimately the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiemerSimon1997106_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiemerSimon1997106-261">[258]</a></sup>
</p>

<li>Roessler, Shirley Elson. <i>Out of the Shadows: Women and Politics in the French Revolution, 1789–95</i> (Peter Lang, 1998) <a class="external text" href="https://www.questia.com/read/53103857/out-of-the-shadows-women-and-politics-in-the-french" rel="nofollow">online</a></li></ul>
